The Ruling on Decreasing (Tanqis) Parts or Conditions of Worship
Determining whether removal of a part or condition abrogates the remaining obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tanqis to determine whether the removal of a part or condition from an obligation abrogates the remaining obligation, and critically evaluate the arguments of Fakhr al-Islam and 'Abd al-Jabbar.

Core Idea
Reducing a part or condition abrogates only the removed element; the remaining act stands without new proof.

The concept of tanqis is central to understanding how changes to an act of worship affect its legal continuity. In this passage, the author navigates a complex debate on whether reducing a part or condition of an obligation constitutes abrogation of the entire remaining act. You will trace the arguments and learn the Hanafi preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus
What is unanimously agreed upon? That tanqis is naskh for the removed element (part or condition).
This is the starting point of the discussion.
2
Locate the main dispute
Is tanqis also naskh for the remaining act? Note three views: author’s preferred (no), Fakhr al-Islam (yes), 'Abd al-Jabbar (distinguishes part/condition).
The dispute hinges on whether the remaining obligation changes.
3
Examine the Hanafi argument
Why does the author say it cannot be naskh? Because that would require a new proof for the remaining, which is not always present.
The reducing text itself may imply the survival of the remaining.
4
Analyze the opposing rebuttal
The opponent argues that the remaining’s ruling has changed (from valid only with extra to valid absolutely). The author responds that only the extra part’s obligation is removed.
The change is in the quality, not the essence.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
What practical difference does this make? If standing in prayer is removed, the prayer remains prayer, not a new act. The mufta bihi is the author’s view.
This prevents excessive fragmentation of obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما نقص جزء أو شرط من العبادة وغيرها من الواجبات (فنسخ له) أي لذلك الجزء والشرط المنقوص (اتفاقاً) لصدق الرفع هناك (وهل هو ) أي التنقيص نسخ (لها) أي للعبادة يعني الباقي منها (فالمختار) عند المصنف (لا) أي عدم كونه مسخاً (وقيل: نعم) هو نسخ، وهو الأشبه، قال الإمام فخر الإسلام، كما أن التقييد بعد الإطلاق نسخ كذلك الإطلاق بعد التقييد (و) قال (عبد الجبار) المعتزلي (في) نقص (الجزء نعم) هو نسخ (و) قال (في) نقصان الشرط: (لا أي ليس هو نسخاً، وظاهر هذا الفرق لما كان تحكماً محضاً، قال: ولعله زعم أن النزاع في نسخ المجموع ففصل) فإن انتفاء الجزء يوجب انتفاء الكل وأما انتفاء شرطية الشرط فلا يضر بقاء حقيقة المجموع، لأنه لم يتغير شيء من أركانه (وليس كذلك أي كما زعم (بل) النزاع (نسخ الباقي بعد النقصان، وحينئذ لا فرق بين الشرط والجزء أيضاً كان شرطاً للباقي، فإنه خارج عنه، واعتداده موقوف عليه فافهم لنا : لو كان التنقيص المذكور (نسخاً للباقي لافتقر إلى دليل لإيجاب الباقي، لأن الدليل الدال عليه قد ارتفع بالنسخ، فلو لم يحتج إلى دليل (آخر) لثبت الحكم من غير دليل (وهو باطل اتفاقاً) ولعلك تقول : لعل النص الدال على التنقيص يدل على بقاء الباقي، كيف لا والقصر والنقصان وأمثاله إنما يدل على بقاء ما وراء المنقوص، ولو كان المنقص بحيث لا يدل على القصر والنقصان بل على الرفع، فيلزم الاحتياج إلى دليل آخر، كما إذا نسخ الوجوب فيحتاج عند مشايخنا الكرام في بقاء الجواز إلى دليل زائد، ودعوى الإجماع مطالب بتصحيحها، ثم إنه لو سلم له أنه ليس بنسخ فنقول : يلزم عليكم أيضاً الحاجة إلى الدليل في بقاء الباقي، فإن النص الدال على المجموع قد ارتفع بورود النسخ، وقد خرج من أن يكون دليلاً، فبقاء الباقي من أي دليل فإن كان هو الدليل الأول فهو إنما كان يدل على وجوبه في ضمن وجوب الكل لا الوجوب استقلالاً، كما مر من في فصل الحكم الاعتراف به أيضاً، فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا، فإذن لا بد من التشبث بالدليل الرافع، فبمثله يقول معشر القائلين بانتساخ الباقي فتأمل تأملاً صادقاً، قائلو كون التنقيص نسخاً (قالوا : ثبت (حرمتها حال كونها بلا جزء أو شرط قبل ورود هذا المنقص والأولى أن يقال : ثبت عدم الاعتداد بها بلا جزء أو شرط ثم ارتفعت الحرمة بالنقص فهو رفع لحكم شرعي ثابت وهو النسخ (قلنا: حرمتها بدونه معناه وجوبه فيها فارتفاع الحرمة بدونه ارتفاع وجوبه فاللازم (نسخه لا نسخ الباقي والكلام) في نسخ الباقي) ولك أن تقرر الدليل هكذا كان الباقي غير مجزىء إلا حال الاقتران بالجزء أو الشرط، والآن صار مجزئاً مطلقاً، فقد تغير حكم الباقي، وهو ضروري، نعم كونه مجزئاً حال المقارنة ملازم لكونه واجباً فيه، لا أنه عينه، كيف ووجوبه صفته ووجوب الباقي مطلقاً صفة له، فكيف يكون أحدهما هو الآخر، وإن شئت قلت: كان الباقي غير مقصود بالإيجاب قبل التنقيص، بل كان واجباً في ضمن إيجاب الكل ولم يكن عبادة مستقلة، إنما كان العبادة الكل والآن بعد التنقيص صار الباقي مقصوداً بالإيجاب وعبادة مستقلة فحدث في الباقي حكم لم يكن وارتفع حكم قد كان، وهو النسخ فتدبر وقد يجاب بأن الزائل) ههنا وجوب الزيادة فهي) أي العبادة باقية (على الجواز الأصلي المفروض، ولم يتجدد وجوب، بل أبطل الوجوب فقط، والثابت هو الوجوب الأول (فارتفع حكم شرعي لا إلى حكم شرعي فلا يكون نسخاً، كذا في «شرح المختصر، وفيه أنه مع مخالفته لجواز النسخ لا إلى بدل كما هو المختار كما سابقا، وهذا مر لا يكون رداً عليه، لأنه إنما جوز النسخ لا إلى بدل هو تكليف لا مطلقاً كما مر (و) مع (صدق تعريفات النسخ عليه فإنه رفع لحكم شرعي (منقوض بالمنقوص) فإنه قد انتسخ بهذا التنقيص مع أنه لا إلى بدل حكم شرعي (فافهم) ووجه المصنف بإرجاعه إلى الجواب الأول بأن المراد أن الزائل وجوب الزيادة، وهو رفع حكم شرعي فيها لا في الباقي، بل هو باق على وجوبه كما كان فلا نسخ فافهم، وتذكر ما أوردنا عليه سابقاً من أنه رفع حكماً في الباقي وهو اتفاقاً، وجوبه مع الزيادة، وقد تغير إلى الوجوب مطلقاً فافهم وتوكل على الله فإنه أعلم بأسراره .
As for the reduction of a part or condition from worship and other obligations, it is abrogation for it, i.e., for that reduced part or condition, unanimously, because removal is applicable there. And is this reduction abrogation of it, i.e., of the worship meaning the remaining part? The chosen view of the author is no, that it is not abrogation. And it was said: Yes, it is abrogation, and it is more likely. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: Just as restriction after absoluteness is abrogation, so also absoluteness after restriction is abrogation. And Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili said regarding reduction of a part: Yes, it is abrogation, and regarding reduction of a condition: No, it is not abrogation. The apparent of this distinction is pure arbitrariness. He said: Perhaps he thought that the dispute is about abrogation of the whole, so he differentiated. For the absence of a part necessitates the absence of the whole, whereas the absence of the conditionality of a condition does not harm the survival of the reality of the whole, because nothing of its pillars has changed. But it is not as he claimed; rather, the dispute is about abrogation of the remaining after reduction. In that case, there is no difference between condition and part, for it is a condition for the remaining, as it is outside it, and its counting is dependent upon it, so understand. For us: If the mentioned reduction were abrogation of the remaining, it would need a proof for the obligation of the remaining, because the proof indicating it has been removed by abrogation. So if it did not need another proof, the ruling would be established without proof, and that is false unanimously. You might say: Perhaps the text indicating the reduction indicates the survival of the remaining? Why not? And shortening and reduction and their like only indicate the survival of what is beyond the reduced. And if the reduced is such that it does not indicate shortening and reduction but rather removal, then the need for another proof follows, just as when obligation is abrogated, according to our respected masters, the survival of permissibility needs an additional proof. And the claim of consensus requires verification. Further, even if it is conceded that it is not abrogation, we say: You also need a proof for the survival of the remaining, because the text indicating the whole has been removed by the arrival of abrogation, and it has ceased to be a proof. So from what proof does the survival of the remaining come? If it is the first proof, it only indicated its obligation within the obligation of the whole, not the obligation independently, as also admitted in the chapter on ruling. So whatever is your answer is our answer. Therefore, one must grasp at the removing proof. In the same way, the group that say the remaining is abrogated speak. So ponder sincerely. Those who hold that the reduction is abrogation said: It is established that its prohibition when it is without a part or condition before the arrival of this reduction — and it is better to say: It is established that it is not considered valid without a part or condition. Then the prohibition was removed by reduction, so it is a removal of an established legal ruling, which is abrogation. We said: Its prohibition without it means its obligation in it, so the removal of the prohibition without it is the removal of its obligation. So the necessary consequence is its abrogation, not the abrogation of the remaining. And the discussion is about the abrogation of the remaining. And you may formulate the proof thus: The remaining was not valid except in conjunction with the part or condition, and now it has become valid absolutely. So the ruling of the remaining has changed, and that is necessary. Yes, its being valid upon conjunction is concomitant with its being obligatory in it, not that it is the same. How? Its obligation is its attribute, and the obligation of the remaining absolutely is an attribute for it. So how can one be the other? And if you wish, you could say: The remaining was not intended for obligation before the reduction, but was obligatory within the obligation of the whole and was not an independent act of worship; the whole was the act of worship. Now after the reduction, the remaining has become intended for obligation and an independent act of worship. So there occurred in the remaining a ruling that did not exist, and a ruling that existed was removed, and that is abrogation. So reflect. And it may be answered that what is removed here is the obligation of the extra part, so it, i.e., the worship, remains upon the original permissibility assumed, and no new obligation arises; rather, the obligation is merely annulled, and what is established is the first obligation. So a legal ruling is removed without a legal ruling in its place, so it is not abrogation. This is as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. In it, despite its opposition to the permissibility of abrogation without replacement as is the chosen view as earlier, and this is not a refutation of it, because he only permitted abrogation without replacement in the case of obligation (taklif), not absolutely as before. And along with the truth of the definitions of abrogation upon it, because it is a removal of a legal ruling, it is refuted by the reduced part, for it has been abrogated by this reduction even though it is without replacement of a legal ruling. So understand. And the author explained by referring to the first answer: The intended meaning is that what is removed is the obligation of the extra part, and it is a removal of a legal ruling in it, not in the remaining; rather, the remaining remains upon its obligation as it was, so no abrogation. So understand. And remember what we previously objected to it: that it removes a ruling in the remaining, which is unanimously its obligation with the extra part, and it has changed to obligation absolutely. So understand and trust in God, for He is the Knower of His secrets.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an in-depth discussion on whether reducing (tanqis) a part or condition of an act of worship constitutes abrogation (naskh) of the remaining obligation. The author first states that unanimously, reducing a part or condition is naskh for that specific part/condition. The dispute is whether it also abrogates the remaining act (e.g., if a condition is removed, does the act become a different obligation?). The author's preferred view is that it is not naskh for the remaining. He cites Imam Fakhr al-Islam's view that it is, and 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili's distinction between parts and conditions. The author rejects that distinction, saying both affect the remaining. He then presents the argument that if the reduction were naskh of the remaining, the original evidence for the remaining would be abrogated, requiring a new evidence, which is problematic. He rebuts that the reducing text itself might imply the survival of the remaining, and engages in a detailed dialectical argument. Eventually, the author leans towards the view that the reduction does not abrogate the remaining, but the worship stays valid without the part/condition perhaps, though the discussion is nuanced. The commentary should highlight the Hanafi position that the removal of an element does not necessarily change the essential nature of the remaining obligation, but it does involve a change in ruling for the remaining (from obligatory with extra to obligatory alone). The author concludes with a note of trust in God.

Key Terms
تَنْقِيص (tanqis): Reduction by removing a part or condition from a legal obligation.
نَسْخ (naskh): Abrogation; removal of a legal ruling by a later text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is abrogation of the entire act of worship.
B) It is abrogation of that part or condition itself.
C) It is specification (takhsis) of the act.
D) It has no effect on the ruling.

Key Takeaway
Reduction of a part or condition abrogates only that element; the remaining obligation stands without requiring a new proof.

How Abrogation is Known: Text and Consensus
A concluding statement listing two definitive proofs for identifying an abrogating legal ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two means by which the abrogating ruling (nasikh) is identified according to Hanafi usul and apply the rule to a given hadith example.

Core Idea
Naskh is not conjectured—it is proven by explicit text or scholarly consensus.

This concluding section (khatima) of our text lays out the foundational rule for identifying an abrogating ruling. The author presents two authoritative sources: the explicit statement of the Lawgiver (the Prophet ﷺ) and the consensus (ijmaʿ) of the scholars. Understanding this rule is crucial because naskh changes a legal ruling, and such a change can only be established by clear evidence, not mere speculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources
Locate in the Arabic text the two ways to know the abrogator: 'النص من الشارع' and 'الإجماع'. Memorize them.
The text uses 'يعرف الناسخ' twice.
2
Analyze the hadith example
Read the Prophetic hadith: 'I used to forbid you from visiting graves; now visit them.' Identify which part is the abrogating statement (nasikh) and which is the abrogated (mansukh).
The phrase 'ألا فزوروها' is the nasikh.
3
Understand consensus as proof
The text mentions that consensus also indicates naskh. Consider why consensus is considered a valid source: the ummah's agreement preserves the correct understanding.
Consensus is a primary source in Hanafi usul.

And among it is his saying, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and Companions: 'I used to forbid you from visiting graves; now visit them. And I used to forbid you from storing the meat of sacrificial animals for more than three days; now keep it as long as you wish. Commentary
This is a concluding section (khatima) that lists the ways to identify an abrogating ruling (nasikh). The first way is explicit textual statement from the Lawgiver (the Prophet ﷺ). The author gives an example of a hadith where the Prophet explicitly revokes previous prohibitions regarding visiting graves, storing sacrificial meat, and drinking from various vessels. The second way is consensus (ijma') of the scholars on the abrogation. The text is cut off at the end, but the point is clear. This is a foundational rule in usul al-fiqh: naskh is not determined by mere speculation but by clear evidence.

Key Terms
نَّاسِخ (nasikh): The abrogating ruling or text that replaces a previous ruling.
مَنْسُوخ (mansukh): The abrogated ruling that has been replaced.
إِجْمَاع (ijmaʿ): Consensus of the scholars, a source of law and a means to identify abrogation.

Key Takeaway
The two definitive sources for identifying an abrogating ruling are explicit text from the Lawgiver and scholarly consensus.

Naskh — Abrogation

Does Reduction (Tanqis) Constitute Abrogation?
Dispute over whether reducing a legal ruling is naskh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that tanqis does not constitute naskh and distinguish between abrogation of a part and abrogation of the whole.

Core Idea
Reduction removes the obligation of the added part only; the remainder's obligation remains unchanged — this is not naskh.

This dispute in usul al-fiqh asks whether reducing a ruling by removing a condition or component abrogates the entire ruling. The Hanafis argue it does not; only the removed part's obligation is abrogated. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying naskh theory and avoiding conflating partial revision with total abrogation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: Does reduction (tanqis) of a legal ruling constitute abrogation (naskh)? Read the passage to pinpoint the two opposing positions.
The issue is about the remainder's ruling after a part is removed.
2
State the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that reducing a ruling changes the remainder's status from conditional to absolute, which is a clear change in the established ruling, hence naskh.
They claim the remainder's ruling was 'conditional' and now becomes 'absolute'.
3
State the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the change is only in the removed part's obligation; the remainder's obligation persists as it was. The removal of the condition abrogates only the condition's requirement, not the whole.
Key phrase: 'فاللازم نسخه لا نسخ الباقي' – it is abrogation of the addition, not the remainder.
4
Analyze the key distinction
The Hanafi argument hinges on distinguishing the obligation of the addition from the obligation of the remainder. The remainder was never independent before; now it is, but its essential obligation is unchanged.
The remainder becomes independently wajib, but that is a new ruling, not abrogation of the old.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Consider an act that was wajib only with a specific condition. If the condition is removed, the act remains wajib. The condition's obligation was abrogated, not the act's obligation.
This shows the thamarah al-khilaf: under the opponent's view, the whole obligation would be lifted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قائلوا كون التنقيص نسخاً (قالوا : ثبت (حرمتها حال كونها بلا جزء أو شرط قبل ورود هذا المنقص والأولى أن يقال : ثبت عدم الاعتداد بها بلا جزء أو شرط ثم ارتفعت الحرمة بالنقص فهو رفع لحكم شرعي ثابت وهو النسخ (قلنا: حرمتها بدونه معناه وجوبه فيها فارتفاع الحرمة بدونه ارتفاع وجوبه فاللازم (نسخه لا نسخ الباقي والكلام) في نسخ الباقي) ولك أن تقرر الدليل هكذا كان الباقي غير مجزىء إلا حال الاقتران بالجزء أو الشرط، والآن صار مجزئاً مطلقاً، فقد تغير حكم الباقي، وهو ضروري، نعم كونه مجزئاً حال المقارنة ملازم لكونه واجباً فيه، لا أنه عينه، كيف ووجوبه صفته ووجوب الباقي مطلقاً صفة له، فكيف يكون أحدهما هو الآخر، وإن شئت قلت: كان الباقي غير مقصود بالإيجاب قبل التنقيص، بل كان واجباً في ضمن إيجاب الكل ولم يكن عبادة مستقلة، إنما كان العبادة الكل والآن بعد التنقيص صار الباقي مقصوداً بالإيجاب وعبادة مستقلة فحدث في الباقي حكم لم يكن وارتفع حكم قد كان، وهو النسخ فتدبر وقد يجاب بأن الزائل) ههنا وجوب الزيادة فهي) أي العبادة باقية (على الجواز الأصلي المفروض، ولم يتجدد وجوب، بل أبطل الوجوب فقط، والثابت هو الوجوب الأول (فارتفع حكم شرعي لا إلى حكم شرعي فلا يكون نسخاً، كذا في «شرح المختصر، وفيه أنه مع مخالفته لجواز النسخ لا إلى بدل كما هو المختار كما سابقا، وهذا مر لا يكون رداً عليه، لأنه إنما جوز النسخ لا إلى بدل هو تكليف لا مطلقاً كما مر (و) مع (صدق تعريفات النسخ عليه فإنه رفع لحكم شرعي (منقوض بالمنقوص) فإنه قد انتسخ بهذا التنقيص مع أنه لا إلى بدل حكم شرعي (فافهم) ووجه المصنف بإرجاعه إلى الجواب الأول بأن المراد أن الزائل وجوب الزيادة، وهو رفع حكم شرعي فيها لا في الباقي، بل هو باق على وجوبه كما كان فلا نسخ فافهم، وتذكر ما أوردنا عليه سابقاً من أنه رفع حكماً في الباقي وهو اتفاقاً، وجوبه مع الزيادة، وقد تغير إلى الوجوب مطلقاً فافهم وتوكل على الله فإنه أعلم بأسراره .
Those who say that reduction is abrogation said: 'It is established that it was prohibited when without a part or condition before the arrival of this reduction, and it is better to say: it is established that it was not considered valid without a part or condition, then the prohibition was removed by the reduction, so it is a removal of an established legal ruling, which is abrogation.' We said: 'Its prohibition without it means its obligation in it, so the removal of the prohibition without it is the removal of its obligation, so what is necessary is its abrogation, not the abrogation of the remainder, and the discussion is about the abrogation of the remainder.' You may present the proof thus: The remainder was not acceptable except when combined with the part or condition, and now it has become acceptable absolutely, so the ruling of the remainder has changed, which is necessary. Yes, its being acceptable when combined is concomitant with its being obligatory in it, not that it is the same. How? Its obligation is its attribute, and the absolute obligation of the remainder is an attribute of it, so how can one be the other? If you wish, say: The remainder was not intended for obligation before the reduction, rather it was obligatory within the obligation of the whole, and it was not an independent act of worship; the act of worship was the whole. Now after the reduction, the remainder has become intended for obligation and an independent act of worship, so a ruling occurred in the remainder that was not there, and a ruling that existed was removed, and that is abrogation. So reflect. It may be answered that what has perished here is the obligation of the addition, so it (i.e., the act of worship) remains on the original permissibility assumed, and no new obligation occurred; rather the obligation was only nullified, and what is established is the first obligation. So an established legal ruling was removed not to another legal ruling, so it is not abrogation, as in the commentary on al-Mukhtasar. But there is in it that despite its opposition to the permissibility of abrogation without a substitute, as is the chosen view as earlier, this does not refute it, because he only permitted abrogation without a substitute for a command, not absolutely, as earlier. And with the applicability of definitions of abrogation to it, because it is the removal of an established legal ruling, it is refuted by the reduced, because with this reduction an established legal ruling has been abrogated without a substitute. So understand. And the author explained by referring it to the first answer: that what is meant is that what perished is the obligation of the addition, and it is a removal of a legal ruling in it, not in the remainder; rather the remainder remains on its obligation as it was, so there is no abrogation. So understand, and remember what we previously objected to him: that he removed a ruling in the remainder, which is unanimously its obligation with the addition, and it has changed to absolute obligation. So understand, and rely on God, for He is most knowing of His secrets.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within usul al-fiqh about whether a reduction (tanqis) in a legal ruling constitutes abrogation (naskh). The opponents argue that reducing a ruling changes the legal status of the remainder, so it is naskh. The Hanafi position, detailed here, is that reduction is not naskh of the remainder; rather, only the obligation of the added part is abrogated. The Hanafi argue that the original obligation included the addition as a condition, and when that condition is removed, the remainder's obligation remains unchanged in itself. The remainder becomes independently obligatory, but that is a new ruling, not abrogation of the old ruling. The text refutes opponents by showing that the removal of the obligation of the addition is naskh in that part, not in the remainder. The author concludes by affirming the Hanafi position and urging reliance on God.

Key Terms
تنقيص (tanqis): Reduction or removal of part of a legal ruling, such as removing a condition or component.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation: removal of an established legal ruling by a later legal evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is abrogated when a ruling is reduced by removing a condition?
A) The entire obligation of the act.
B) Only the obligation of the part or condition that was removed.
C) The permissibility of the act.
D) The remainder of the act becomes obligatory.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, tanqis (reduction) abrogates only the obligation of the removed part, not the remainder; the original obligation of the remainder persists as before.

Methods of Identifying the Abrogator (Nasikh) in Hanafi Usul
Khatima listing ways to identify the nasikh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main methods for recognizing the nasikh in Hanafi usul and evaluate the Hanafi acceptance of a companion's statement 'this is nasikh' against the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
A companion's identification of nasikh is accepted as knowledge of chronology, not ijtihad, in Hanafi usul.

In the study of naskh, one of the most critical skills is knowing which evidence abrogates which. Hanafi usul provides several reliable methods. The most debated is whether we can accept a companion's direct statement 'this is nasikh'. This passage from the khatima of the usul text lays out the approved methods and defends the Hanafi position against Shafi'i objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three explicit methods
List the methods mentioned: (1) explicit text from the Lawgiver (nass), (2) consensus (ijma), (3) establishing chronology (dabt al-tawarikh). Recognize that the companion's statement about chronology is a subset of the third method.
The hadith of visiting graves is an example of explicit nasikh from the Prophet.
2
Analyze the dispute over the companion's statement 'this is nasikh'
Understand the Hanafi position: accept it as proof. Understand the Shafi'i position: reject it as potential ijtihad. Identify the key difference: Hanafis view it as transmission, Shafi'is view it as opinion.
The Hanafi response compares it to testimony like witnesses for stoning or midwife testimony.
3
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
Examine the Hanafi argument: the companion's integrity is certain, and the identification of nasikh requires knowledge of chronology and context, not ijtihad. Therefore, the report is reliable.
The text explicitly states 'there is no room for opinion (ra'y) in it'.
4
Distinguish valid from invalid indicators
Note that the order of the mus'haf and the relative age of a companion are NOT valid methods to determine nasikh. The text explicitly rejects both.
Reason: mus'haf order is not chronological; age does not guarantee sequence of hearing.

خاتمة : يعرف الناسخ بالنص من الشارع (ومنه قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «كنت نهيتكم عن زيارة القبور ألا فزوروها ونهيتكم عن ادخار لحوم الأضاحي فوق ثلاث فأمسكوها ما بدا لكم ونهيتكم عن النبيذ إلا المنسوخ فيكون خطأ. وكذا عمل الصحابي على خلاف نص مفسر بالناسخ، لأن عدالته مقطوعة (و) يعرف الناسخ (بضبط التاريخ فيحكم بناسخية المؤخر عند التعارض فيقبل قول الصحابي هذا سابق لأنه إخبار عدل بخبر لا مساغ ملزماً للغير، وذلك لأنه بعيد، فإن شأن العدل أن يهتم في أمر النسخ فلا يحكم إلا عن بصيرة كما بينا (لكن) الشافعية قالوا: إذا تعارض متواتران فقالوا أي الصحابة، لكن إذا لم يبلغوا أحد التواتر، والأظهر قال، بصيغة المفرد أو جمع على قصد التعظيم هذا ناسخ احتمل الرد لرجوعه إلى نسخ المتواتر بالآحاد روايته) وهي رواية أنه ناسخ (و) احتمل (القبول) أيضاً (فلعل) أي لأنه لعل الناسخ المتواتر) الذي قال فيه الصحابي : هذا ناسخ (والآحاد) الذي هو روايته (دليله) أي دليل النسخ (وما لا يقبل ابتداء قد يقبل مالا كشاهدي (الإحصان فإنها لا تقبل ابتداء، وتقبل لترتب الرجم : كما في شهادة القابلة فإنها لا تقبل لإثبات النسب ابتداء، لكن تقبل في إثبات الولادة ثم يترتب عليه النسب، فكذا ههنا، يقبل قول الواحد لإثبات النسخ والناسخ هو المتواتر وإن كان لا ينسخ ابتداء (قيل) في حواشي ميرزاجان» (فيه أن قول الصحابي ذلك أي إخباره بالنسخ (جاز أن يكون اجتهاداً لا نقلاً عنه عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام) واجتهاده غير ملزم لمجتهد آخر على ما يراه الشافعية أقول في (المتواترين إذا تعارضا النسخ لازم لا عن اجتهاد وإلا لزم كون أحدهما خطأ فمن الصحابي) الإخبار بالنسخ ليس إلا بيان السبق فما حكم عليه بالناسخية مؤخر والآخر مقدم (وهو) أي إخباره وبيانه السبق اتفاق قبولاً، فإن قلت: لعل الصحابي إنما حكم بالنسخ لظنهما متعارضين اجتهاداً؟ قلت : ) أولاً: ظنه التعارض لا يكون إلا لتعين المعنى عنده بالسماع أو مشاهدة القرائن فيقبل. وثانياً : أن حكمه بالنسخ إنما يكون لعلمه بالسبق واللحوق فيقبل قوله فيه، فإن ظهر التعارض يحكم بالنسخ والكلام فيه ومن ههنا تبين أن (القبول أي قبول إخباره بالنسخ (هو المقبول وابن الحاجب محجوب عنه فتوقف فيه ولم يكن له ذلك (ثم لا تعرف الناسخية (بالبعدية في المصحف) فيستدل به على البعدية في النزول، لأن ترتيب المصحف وإن كان من عند الله تعالى لكنه ليس على ترتيب النزول، وهو ظاهر جداً، كيف وسورة اقرأ أول القرآن نزولاً وكتب في قريب من الآخر وسورة البقرة مع كونها مدنية كتبت في الأول وسورة البراءة آخر المنزلات كتبت مقدمة على أكثر المكيات (ولا) يعرف تعيين الناسخ (بحداثة سن الصحابي) أي الراوي فيحكم بأن مرويه متأخر عن مروي من هو أسن، فلعل الأسن إنما سمع التي هي
Conclusion: The abrogator is known by text from the Lawgiver, including His saying, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions: 'I used to forbid you from visiting graves, but now visit them; I used to forbid you from storing sacrificial meat for more than three days, but now keep it as long as you wish; I used to forbid you from nabidh except in a waterskin, but now drink from all waterskins, but do not drink intoxicants.' Narrated by Muslim without the word 'kuntu'. (And) the abrogator is known by consensus, as when there is consensus on the ruling of a text that contradicts another text; for if the text upon which there is consensus were not abrogating, then consensus on the abrogated would be erroneous. Likewise, the action of a companion contrary to an explicit text is interpreted as abrogation, because his integrity is certain. (And) the abrogator is known by establishing chronology, so the later one is judged as abrogating when there is conflict. Thus the statement of a companion 'this is earlier' is accepted because it is the report of a trustworthy person about a matter with no room for opinion. As for his statement 'this is abrogating', according to the Hanafis it is accepted, but not according to the Shafi'is. They said: because identifying the abrogator may be through ijtihad, so it is not a proof for others. We said: because the identification by a trustworthy person whose integrity is certain can only be through knowledge of chronology and conflict, for what is intended by him is known by observing indications, so his judgment of abrogation is based on insight, and there is no room for ijtihad in it. It is also refuted that perhaps he judged abrogation due to his interpretation of the narrated text in a meaning conflicting with the first through ijtihad, so it would not be binding on others. That is far-fetched, for it is the nature of a trustworthy person to be careful in matters of abrogation and not judge except with insight, as we explained. However, the Shafi'is said: When two mutawatir texts conflict, they say 'any of the companions', but if they do not reach the level of tawatur, the preferable opinion is that the statement of a single person or a group intending glorification 'this is abrogating' may be rejected because it involves abrogation of mutawatir by ahad narration—his narration being that it is abrogating—and it may also be accepted because perhaps the abrogating (mutawatir) text which the companion said is abrogating, and the ahad (narration) is its evidence. And what is not accepted initially may be accepted secondarily, like the two witnesses of ihsan: they are not accepted initially but are accepted for establishing stoning. Similarly, the testimony of the midwife is not accepted for establishing lineage initially, but is accepted for establishing birth, then lineage follows from that. So here, the statement of one person is accepted to establish abrogation, while the abrogating text is mutawatir, even though it cannot abrogate initially. It was said in the glosses of Mirzajan: 'There is in it that the companion's statement, i.e., his report of abrogation, may be ijtihad, not a narration from him (peace be upon him), and his ijtihad is not binding on another mujtahid according to the Shafi'is.' I say: In the case of two conflicting mutawatir texts, abrogation is necessary, not from ijtihad, otherwise one of them would be false. So the companion's report of abrogation is only an explanation of precedence, so he ruled on abrogation: the later and the other earlier. And his report and explanation of precedence are accepted by agreement. If you say: perhaps the companion only judged abrogation because he thought they conflicted through ijtihad? I say: first, his thought of conflict only occurs because the meaning is established for him through hearing or observing indications, so it is accepted. Second, his judgment of abrogation is only due to his knowledge of precedence and subsequence, so his statement is accepted therein. If conflict appears, he judges abrogation, and the discussion is therein. From here it becomes clear that acceptance—i.e., acceptance of his report of abrogation—is the accepted view, and Ibn al-Hajib is veiled from it, so he hesitated and had no right to do so. Then abrogation is not known by later placement in the mus'haf, as evidence for later revelation, because the order of the mus'haf, though from Allah, is not according to the order of revelation—this is very clear. How? Surah 'Iqra' was the first revealed yet is written near the end, and Surah al-Baqara, though Madani, is written at the beginning, and Surah al-Bara'a, the last revealed, is written before most Makki surahs. Nor is the identification of the abrogator known by the youthfulness of the companion's age, i.e., the narrator, so that his narration is judged as later than that of an older one; perhaps the older one heard which is...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This conclusion (khatima) of the usul text outlines the methods by which the abrogating ruling (nasikh) is identified. The first method is explicit textual statement from the Lawgiver, exemplified by the hadith where the Prophet explicitly abrogates previous prohibitions. The second method is consensus (ijma): if the community unanimously acts on a text that contradicts another, the consensus indicates that the earlier text is abrogated. The third method is establishing chronology (tawarikh): when two texts conflict, the later one is nasikh. This allows accepting a companion's statement about which text is earlier. The text then discusses the dispute over accepting a companion's statement 'this is nasikh.' The Hanafi position is to accept it, because a trustworthy companion's identification of nasikh is based on knowledge of chronology and context, not mere ijtihad. The Shafi'i position is to reject it, arguing it could be ijtihad and thus not binding. The Hanafis respond by emphasizing the companion's integrity and the lack of room for ijtihad in pure transmission of chronology. They also compare it to accepting testimony for stoning or midwife testimony for lineage, where evidence accepted secondarily even if not initially. Finally, the text clarifies that the order of the mus'haf is not a reliable indicator of chronological order, nor is the relative age of a companion (i.e., younger vs. older narrator) a valid method to determine abrogation.

Key Terms
ناسخ (nasikh): The abrogating ruling or evidence that removes a previous ruling.
منسوخ (mansukh): The abrogated ruling that is removed by a later evidence.
ضبط التاريخ (dabt al-tawarikh): Establishing the chronological order of evidence to determine which is later (nasikh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is a companion's statement 'this is nasikh' accepted?
A) Because the companion's ijtihad is binding on all mujtahids.
B) Because the companion's report is based on knowledge of chronology and context, not mere opinion.
C) Because all companions are infallible.
D) Because Shafi'is also accept it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a companion's identification of nasikh is accepted as reliable testimony of chronology, not as ijtihad, and is a valid method to resolve contradictory evidence.

Naskh — Abrogation
advanced
8 min
Companion's Statement on Naskh: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute on the probative value of a Companion's statement about naskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on accepting a Companion's statement 'this is nasikh' as proof and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A Companion's designation of naskh is based on historical knowledge, not ijtihad, thus it is binding.

When a Companion says 'this hadith abrogates that one,' can we take it as definitive? The Hanafis argue yes, because the Companion's integrity is certain and identifying naskh requires knowledge of chronology—not personal reasoning. The Shafi'is object that the Companion might be exercising ijtihad. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response and the analogies used to support it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed claim
Determine the exact point of disagreement: Is a Companion's statement 'this is nasikh' a binding proof for naskh?
This is an usul dispute about the evidentiary value of a Companion's report.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafis accept the statement because the Companion's 'adala (integrity) is certain (maktu') and designating naskh requires knowledge of historical sequence, not ijtihad.
Key: 'عدالته مقطوعة' – the probity is not merely presumed but certain.
3
Present the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'is argue that specifying the nasikh may involve ijtihad, so it is not binding on others.
The Shafi'i argument turns on the possibility of personal reasoning in determining naskh.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis rebut that the Companion's judgment is based on direct observation or reliable hearing (not opinion), and the probability of error is negligible. They also support this with an analogy: solitary testimony is accepted for derivative rulings (e.g., witnesses for stoning, midwife for birth).
Note the analogy: just as solitary testimony proves a derivative, the Companion's statement proves naskh even if it relates to a mutawatir text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الأول/ الكتاب مع علمه به [؟] علمه حكم نص معارض لنص آخر فإنه لو لم يكن النص المجمع عليه ناسخاً لكان الإجماع على المنسوخ فيكون خطأ. وكذا عمل الصحابي على خلاف نص مفسر بالناسخ، لأن عدالته مقطوعة (و) يعرف الناسخ (بضبط التاريخ فيحكم بناسخية المؤخر عند التعارض فيقبل قول الصحابي هذا سابق لأنه إخبار عدل بخبر لا مساغ فيه للرأي (أما قوله هذا ناسخ فعند الحنفية مقبول (لا) (الشافعية قالوا : لأن تعيين الناسخ قد يكون عن اجتهاد فلا يكون حجة على الغير، قلنا : لأن تعيين العدل الموثوق بعدالته بل مقطوعها لناسخ لا يكون إلا عن علم بالتاريخ والتعارض، فإن المراد عنده معلوم بمشاهدة القرائن، فحكمه بالنسخ عن بصيرة، ولا مجال للاجتهاد فيه فاندفع أيضاً أنه لعله إنما حكم بالنسخ لحمله المروي على معنى معارض للأول عن اجتهاد فلا يكون ملزماً للغير، وذلك لأنه بعيد، فإن شأن العدل أن يهتم في أمر النسخ فلا يحكم إلا عن بصيرة كما بينا (لكن) الشافعية قالوا: إذا تعارض متواتران فقالوا أي الصحابة، لكن إذا لم يبلغوا أحد التواتر، والأظهر قال، بصيغة المفرد أو جمع على قصد التعظيم هذا ناسخ احتمل الرد لرجوعه إلى نسخ المتواتر بالآحاد روايته) وهي رواية أنه ناسخ (و) احتمل (القبول) أيضاً (فلعل) أي لأنه لعل الناسخ المتواتر) الذي قال فيه الصحابي : هذا ناسخ (والآحاد) الذي هو روايته (دليله) أي دليل النسخ (وما لا يقبل ابتداء قد يقبل مالا كشاهدي الإحصان فإنها لا تقبل ابتداء، وتقبل لترتب الرجم : كما في شهادة القابلة فإنها لا تقبل لإثبات النسب ابتداء، لكن تقبل في إثبات الولادة ثم يترتب عليه النسب، فكذا ههنا، يقبل قول الواحد لإثبات النسخ والناسخ هو المتواتر وإن كان لا ينسخ ابتداء (قيل) في حواشي ميرزاجان (فيه أن قول الصحابي ذلك أي إخباره بالنسخ (جاز أن يكون اجتهاداً لا نقلاً عنه عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام) واجتهاده غير ملزم لمجتهد آخر على ما يراه الشافعية أقول في (المتواترين إذا تعارضا النسخ لازم لا عن اجتهاد وإلا لزم كون أحدهما خطأ فمن الصحابي) الإخبار بالنسخ ليس إلا بيان السبق فما حكم عليه بالناسخية مؤخر والآخر مقدم (وهو) أي إخباره وبيانه السبق اتفاق قبولاً، فإن قلت: لعل الصحابي إنما حكم بالنسخ لظنهما متعارضين اجتهاداً؟ قلت : أولاً: ظنه التعارض لا يكون إلا لتعين المعنى عنده بالسماع أو مشاهدة القرائن فيقبل. وثانياً : أن حكمه بالنسخ إنما يكون لعلمه بالسبق واللحوق فيقبل قوله فيه، فإن ظهر التعارض يحكم بالنسخ والكلام فيه ومن ههنا تبين أن (القبول أي قبول إخباره بالنسخ (هو المقبول وابن الحاجب محجوب عنه فتوقف فيه ولم يكن له ذلك
The first principle: the book, with his knowledge meaning his knowledge. The ruling of a text opposing another text: if the text agreed upon by consensus were not abrogating, then consensus on the abrogated would be error. Similarly, the action of a Companion contrary to an explicit text is interpreted as abrogating, because his integrity is certain. And the nasikh is known by recording history, so the later is judged as abrogating when conflicting, and the Companion's statement 'this is earlier' is accepted because it is the report of an upright person about something not open to opinion. As for his statement 'this is nasikh', among the Hanafis it is accepted; the Shafi'is said: because specifying the nasikh may be by ijtihad, so it is not a proof for others. We said: because the designation by an upright person whose integrity is trusted (indeed certain) for the nasikh can only be from knowledge of history and conflict, for the meaning is known to him by observing indications, so his judgment on abrogation is based on insight, and there is room for ijtihad in it. It is also refuted that perhaps he judged abrogation because he carried the reported text to a meaning opposing the first by ijtihad, thus not binding on others. This is unlikely, for an upright person takes care in the matter of abrogation and only judges from insight as we explained. But the Shafi'is said: if two mutawatir texts conflict and a Companion (or a group intending glorification) says 'this is nasikh', it may be rejected because it would abrogate mutawatir with solitary narration, and may also be accepted, because perhaps the nasikh is the mutawatir and the solitary narration is the evidence for it. And what is not accepted initially may be accepted as evidence, like the two witnesses of ihsan (which are not accepted initially but accepted for establishing stoning) or the midwife's testimony (not accepted to establish lineage initially but accepted to establish birth, then lineage follows). So here, the solitary report is accepted to establish naskh, while the nasikh itself is mutawatir even if it does not abrogate initially. It was said in the glosses of Mirzajan: that the Companion's statement (i.e., his report of naskh) may be ijtihad, not a transmission from him (peace be upon him and his companions), and his ijtihad is not binding on another mujtahid according to the Shafi'is. I say: regarding two mutawatirs that conflict, naskh necessarily arises not from ijtihad, otherwise one of them would be in error. So the Companion's report of naskh is only a clarification of precedence, so what he judges as nasikh is later and the other earlier. And his report and clarification of precedence is unanimously accepted. If you say: perhaps the Companion only judged naskh because he thought they conflicted by ijtihad? I say: first, his assumption of conflict can only be because the meaning is determined by hearing or observing indications, so it is accepted. Second, his judgment of naskh can only be from knowledge of precedence and subsequence, so his statement is accepted. If conflict appears, he judges naskh; the discussion is about that. From here it is clear that acceptance (i.e., acceptance of his report of naskh) is the accepted view, and Ibn al-Hajib is veiled from it, so he hesitated and did not have that.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether a Companion's statement 'this is nasikh' is a proof. The Hanafi position: it is accepted because the Companion's integrity is certain and his designation of naskh can only come from knowledge of historical precedence and textual conflict—not from ijtihad. The Shafi'i position: such designation may be ijtihad, thus not binding. The Hanafi response: the Companion's notice of conflict and judgment of naskh is based on direct observation or hearing, not opinion. The passage also analogizes with accepting solitary testimony for derivative purposes (e.g., witnesses of ihsan for stoning, midwife's testimony for birth). Finally, the author endorses the Hanafi view and criticizes Ibn al-Hajib for hesitation.

Key Terms
ناسخ (nasikh): An abrogating text that removes the ruling of a previously revealed text.
عدالة الصحابي ('adālat al-ṣaḥābī): The integrity of the Companion; the text asserts it is maktu' (certain).
متواتر (mutawātir): A hadith reported by so many narrators that collusion on falsehood is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a Companion's statement 'this is nasikh' accepted as proof?
A) Because the Companion is an imam and his ijtihad is binding.
B) Because the Companion's integrity is certain and his knowledge of history leaves no room for ijtihad in this matter.
C) Because the Companion heard directly from the Prophet.
D) Because the Companion is infallible.

Key Takeaway
A Companion's designation of naskh is accepted because it is based on certain historical knowledge, not personal ijtihad.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
12 min
Invalid Methods for Identifying the Nasikh
Lists invalid methods for identifying nasikh and explains why each fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four invalid criteria for determining which text is nasikh and explain the Hanafi reasoning for rejecting each.

Core Idea
Order in mushaf, narrator age, conversion time, and agreement with original permissibility are all invalid for identifying nasikh.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut systematically refutes four commonly proposed indicators of chronological precedence in naskh. As a student of usul, you must recognize why these criteria fail, and in the case of the fourth, understand the distinction between definitive naskh and practical preference (tarjih).

Guided Path
1
Evaluate the mushaf order criterion
Identify why the order of suras in the written Quran does not indicate chronological order. The text gives clear examples: Surah Al-Alaq (first revealed) is near the end, while Surah Al-Baqara (Medinan) is at the beginning.
This is a consensus among scholars.
2
Examine the narrator's age criterion
Understand that a Companion's young age does not guarantee his narration is later, because an older Companion may have heard the same narration after the younger one.
The older might have heard from the younger.
3
Analyze the conversion time criterion
Realize that a later conversion does not necessarily mean a later narration, unless the narrator explicitly states hearing after converting and the earlier narrator died before that conversion.
Possible hearing before Islam or after the elder's death.
4
Assess the agreement with original permissibility criterion
Work through the argument that a text agreeing with original permissibility is later because it 'establishes' (tasis) rather than 'confirms' (ta'kid). The Hanafis reject this because it would require naskh by ijtihad, which is impermissible. Instead, they treat it as a tarjih (preference) for the contradicting text to avoid repetition of removal.
This is a madhhab dispute; Hanafis delay the contradicting text for practical action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا تعرف الناسخية (بالبعدية في المصحف) فيستدل به على البعدية في النزول، لأن ترتيب المصحف وإن كان من عند الله تعالى لكنه ليس على ترتيب النزول، وهو ظاهر جداً، كيف وسورة اقرأ أول القرآن نزولاً وكتب في قريب من الآخر وسورة البقرة مع كونها مدنية كتبت في الأول وسورة البراءة آخر المنزلات كتبت مقدمة على أكثر المكيات (ولا) يعرف تعيين الناسخ (بحداثة سن الصحابي) أي الراوي فيحكم بأن مرويه متأخر عن مروي من هو أسن، فلعل الأسن إنما سمع بعد سماع الأحدث (ولا) يعرف (بتأخر إسلامه فيحكم بتأخر مرويه عمن تقدم إسلامه لجواز سماعه بعد سماع متأخر الإسلام أو سماع متأخر الإسلام قبل إسلامه، اللهم إلا أن يكون إسلام الأحدث بعد وفاة الأسن وصرح بالسماع أو متأخر الإسلام أسلم بعد وفاة متقدم الإسلام وصرح بالسماع (لا) يعرف (بالموافقة للبراءة الأصلية فيدل على التأخر) عما هو مخالف لها لأن التأسيس خير من التأكيد) ولو تقدم موافق البراءة يكون تأكيداً، فلا بد أن يكون متأخراً عن المخالف، فيكون هو رافعاً، فيكون تأسيساً ثم الموافق يكون ناسخاً إياه فيكون تأسيساً أيضاً (وهو ضعيف، لأنه نسخ بالاجتهاد وهو غير جائز ربما يقال إن النسخ غيره، وبالرأي عرف بالتأخير والرأي مما يهدى إليه، ثم يثبت النسخ ضمناً، وكم من شيء لا يثبت ابتداءً ويثبت ضمناً فتأمل فيه (مع أن كونه مباحاً شرعياً فائدة زائدة) والبراءة الأصلية لا تدل عليه، فلو كان موافقها مقدماً أفاد الإباحة الشرعية فلا تأكيد أصلاً (والحنفية) يخالفون فيه و(يؤخرون المخالف) للبراءة لئلا يتكرر الرفع فإنه لو تقدم المخالف رفع البراءة، ثم الموافق يرفعه فيتكرر الرفع، وفي التعبير بالرفع والعدول عن النسخ إيماء إلى أنه لا يلزم تكرر النسخ، لأن رفع الإباحة الأصلية ليس نسخاً وإيماء إلى أن المراد في عبارات المشايخ رحمهم الله تعالى بتكرر النسخ تكرر الرفع وهذا القدر يكفي ههنا فالمناقشة فيه لا تضرنا، ثم أنه لما كان هذا القول أيضاً نسخاً بالاجتهاد قال : (وهذا القول منهم ليس حكماً بالنسخ في الحقيقة، بل هو (ترجيح) لأحد المتعارضين في التعارض لضرورة العمل (لا تعيين للناسخ) في الحقيقة، ولا شك أن هذا القدر من الترجيح يكفي لترجيح العمل بالمخالف ( فتدبر) ولا تزل فإنه مزلة والله تعالى أعلم بمراد خواص عباده .
Then naskh is not known by (distance in the mushaf) so that it is used to infer later revelation, because the order of the mushaf, though from Allah, is not according to the order of revelation. This is very evident: how can Surah Iqra' (Al-Alaq) was first revealed but written near the end, and Surah al-Baqara, though Medinan, is written at the beginning, and Surah al-Bara'a (At-Tawba) was last revealed but written before most Meccan suras. Nor is nasikh known by (the young age of the Companion, i.e., the narrator) so that his narration is judged later than that of an older Companion, because the older may have heard after the younger. Nor is it known by (his later acceptance of Islam) so that his narration is judged later than one who accepted Islam earlier, because it is possible he heard after the later Muslim heard or the later Muslim heard before his Islam, unless the later one's Islam was after the death of the older and he explicitly said he heard, or the later Muslim accepted Islam after the death of the earlier Muslim and explicitly said he heard. Nor is it known by (agreement with original permissibility, which indicates lateness) compared to what contradicts it, because establishing (tasis) is better than confirming (ta'kid). If the agreeing text were earlier, it would be confirmation, so it must be later than the contradicting text, making it the remover, thus it would be establishment. Then the agreeing text would abrogate it, becoming establishment as well. This is weak, because it is naskh by ijtihad, which is not permissible. Perhaps it is said that naskh is something else, and by opinion the delay is known, and opinion can lead to it; then naskh is established indirectly. Many things are not established initially but are established indirectly, so reflect on it. Moreover, the fact that something is permissible (mubah) according to sharia is an additional benefit; original permissibility does not indicate it. If the agreeing text were earlier, it would yield sharia permissibility, so there is no confirmation at all. The Hanafis differ on this and delay the contradicting text over the one agreeing with original permissibility, to avoid repetition of removal. If the contradicting text were earlier, it would remove original permissibility, then the agreeing text would remove that, causing repetition of removal. The expression 'removal' (raf') instead of 'abrogation' (naskh) indicates that repetition of naskh is not necessary, because removal of original permissibility is not naskh. It also indicates that what the scholars mean by 'repetition of naskh' is repetition of removal, and this is sufficient here. So criticism does not harm us. Then, since this view also is naskh by ijtihad, he said: 'This view of theirs is not a judgment of naskh in reality, but rather a preference (tarjih) for one of two conflicting texts due to the necessity of action, not a designation of the nasikh in reality.' There is no doubt that this degree of preference suffices to prefer acting by the contradicting text. So ponder, and do not slip, for it is a slippery place. Allah knows best the intent of His special servants.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage lists invalid methods for identifying the abrogating text (nasikh) and explains why they fail. First, the order in the Quran (mushaf) does not indicate order of revelation, illustrated by well-known examples. Second, the age of the Companion narrator is unreliable because an older Companion may have heard after a younger one. Third, the timing of a Companion's conversion to Islam is not a reliable indicator unless it is clear that the later Muslim heard after the elder's death. Fourth, the argument that a text agreeing with original permissibility (al-bara'a al-asliyya) is later because it establishes rather than confirms is rejected as it would require naskh by ijtihad, which is impermissible. The Hanafi position is to prefer the contradicting text (i.e., the one that restricts or overrides original permissibility) to avoid repetition of removal (raf'). Ultimately, the author notes that this is not a true determination of naskh but a preference (tarjih) for practical purposes.

Key Terms
ترتيب المصحف (tartīb al-muṣḥaf): The order of suras in the written Quran copy, which is not the chronological order of revelation.
البراءة الأصلية (al-barā'a al-aṣliyya): The original state of permissibility (ibaha) before revelation restricts or mandates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the later Companion may have heard the narration before converting, or the earlier Companion may have heard after the later one's conversion
C) Because a later conversion always means the narration is earlier
D) Because the Prophet forbade using personal characteristics

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects four common methods for identifying the nasikh, especially the argument from original permissibility, which it treats as a tarjih, not a true naskh determination.

Tarjih by Opposition to Original Presumption
Explaining that opposing bara'a indicates later narration, and Hanafi response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of tarjih by opposition to the original presumption (al-bara'a al-asliyya) to determine which of two contradictory hadith is preferred for practice, and distinguish this from abrogation (naskh).

Core Idea
When two hadith conflict, the Hanafi school prefers the one opposing bara'a to avoid repeated lifting, deeming it tarjih not naskh.

One of the key challenges in usul al-fiqh is reconciling contradictory hadith. Scholars developed methods to determine which narration is later and thus potentially abrogating. This lesson examines one such criterion: whether agreement or disagreement with the original presumption (bara'a) indicates later transmission. The Hanafis reject using agreement with bara'a and instead prefer the contradictory hadith to avoid repeated lifting, clarifying that this is a practical tarjih, not abrogation by ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Recognize that the problem is how to determine the later of two contradictory hadith when direct historical evidence is lacking. The passage discusses two proposed indicators: later Islam of a narrator and agreement with the original presumption.
The original presumption (bara'a) means things are free from divine ruling until a revealed ruling establishes otherwise.
2
Understand the Rejected Criterion
See why the passage rejects using the later Islam of a narrator to determine the later hadith (due to possibilities of earlier hearing). Also understand the opponent's argument: agreement with bara'a indicates later transmission because establishing a new ruling (ta'sis) is better than mere emphasis (ta'kid).
The opponent's logic: if a hadith agrees with bara'a, it would be emphasizing a ruling already presumed, so the opposing hadith must be earlier, establishing a new ruling.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Critique
The Hanafis argue that the opponent's method would involve abrogation by ijtihad, which is forbidden. Even if naskh is not directly intended, it would be implied, and that is not permissible. The passage also refutes the idea that agreement with bara'a provides an additional benefit of legal permissibility.
Abrogation (naskh) can only occur by divine revelation, not human reasoning.
4
Grasp the Hanafi Alternative
The Hanafis prefer the hadith that opposes bara'a to avoid repeated lifting (raf'). If the opposing hadith were earlier, it would lift the original presumption; then a later agreeing hadith would lift that ruling again, causing repetition. This is undesirable, so the opposing hadith is taken as later.
Lifting (raf') of the original presumption is not considered abrogation because the original presumption is not a revealed ruling.
5
Distinguish Tarjih from Naskh
The passage emphasizes that this Hanafi preference is a tarjih (preponderance) for the purpose of practical action, not a determination of actual abrogation. Therefore, the ruling is considered preferable but not necessarily the definitive abrogator.
This avoids the charge of abrogation by ijtihad; it is simply weighing evidence for action, not declaring which hadith abrogates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد سماع الأحدث (ولا) يعرف (بتأخر إسلامه فيحكم بتأخر مرويه عمن تقدم إسلامه لجواز سماعه بعد سماع متأخر الإسلام أو سماع متأخر الإسلام قبل إسلامه، اللهم إلا أن يكون إسلام الأحدث بعد وفاة الأسن وصرح بالسماع أو متأخر الإسلام أسلم بعد وفاة متقدم الإسلام وصرح بالسماع (لا) يعرف (بالموافقة للبراءة الأصلية فيدل على التأخر) عما هو مخالف لها لأن التأسيس خير من التأكيد) ولو تقدم موافق البراءة يكون تأكيداً، فلا بد أن يكون متأخراً عن المخالف، فيكون هو رافعاً، فيكون تأسيساً ثم الموافق يكون ناسخاً إياه فيكون تأسيساً أيضاً (وهو ضعيف، لأنه نسخ بالاجتهاد وهو غير جائز ربما يقال إن النسخ غيره، وبالرأي عرف بالتأخير والرأي مما يهدى إليه، ثم يثبت النسخ ضمناً، وكم من شيء لا يثبت ابتداءً ويثبت ضمناً فتأمل فيه (مع أن كونه مباحاً شرعياً فائدة زائدة) والبراءة الأصلية لا تدل عليه، فلو كان موافقها مقدماً أفاد الإباحة الشرعية فلا تأكيد أصلاً (والحنفية) يخالفون فيه و(يؤخرون المخالف) للبراءة لئلا يتكرر الرفع فإنه لو تقدم المخالف رفع البراءة، ثم الموافق يرفعه فيتكرر الرفع، وفي التعبير بالرفع والعدول عن النسخ إيماء إلى أنه لا يلزم تكرر النسخ، لأن رفع الإباحة الأصلية ليس نسخاً وإيماء إلى أن المراد في عبارات المشايخ رحمهم الله تعالى بتكرر النسخ تكرر الرفع وهذا القدر يكفي ههنا فالمناقشة فيه لا تضرنا، ثم أنه لما كان هذا القول أيضاً نسخاً بالاجتهاد قال : (وهذا القول منهم ليس حكماً بالنسخ في الحقيقة، بل هو (ترجيح) لأحد المتعارضين في التعارض لضرورة العمل (لا تعيين للناسخ) في الحقيقة، ولا شك أن هذا القدر من الترجيح يكفي لترجيح العمل بالمخالف ( فتدبر) ولا تزل فإنه مزلة والله تعالى أعلم بمراد خواص عباده .
After hearing the later (hadith), it is not known by the later Islam of the narrator to judge the later narration from the one whose Islam is earlier, because it is possible that the later Islam narrator heard after the hearing of the one with later Islam, or that the one with later Islam heard before his Islam. Unless the later Islam happened after the death of the older and explicitly stated hearing, or the one with later Islam became Muslim after the death of the one with earlier Islam and explicitly stated hearing. It is not known by agreement with the original presumption (bara'a) to indicate later (narration) as opposed to the one that contradicts it, because establishment (ta'sis) is better than emphasis (ta'kid). If the one agreeing with bara'a were earlier, it would be emphasis, so it must be later than the contradicting one; then the contradicting one would be the raiser (raf'), making it ta'sis; then the agreeing one would be the abrogator of it, making it also ta'sis. That is weak, because it is abrogation by ijtihad, which is not permissible. It may be said that abrogation is other than that? And by opinion (ra'y) the later is known, and opinion is something that can be guided to; then abrogation is established implicitly. Many things are not established initially but established implicitly. So consider. Also, that it is a permissible (mubah) ruling is an additional benefit; the original presumption does not indicate it. If the agreeing one were earlier, it would provide legal permissibility, so there is no emphasis at all. The Hanafis disagree and delay the contradicting to bara'a to avoid repeated lifting (raf'). For if the contradicting were earlier, it would lift the bara'a, then the agreeing one would lift it, resulting in repeated lifting. The expression 'lifting' and avoiding 'abrogation' indicates that repeated abrogation is not necessary, because lifting the original permissibility is not abrogation, and indicates that what the scholars mean by repeated abrogation is repeated lifting, and this is sufficient here. Then since this statement is also abrogation by ijtihad, he said: 'This statement of theirs is not a ruling of abrogation in reality, but rather it is a tarjih of one of the two contradictories in conflict for necessity of action, not a determination of the abrogator in reality. There is no doubt that this amount of tarjih is sufficient to prefer acting on the contradictory. So ponder, and do not slip, for it is a slippery place, and Allah knows best the intention of His special servants.'
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage discusses the method of determining which of two contradictory hadith is later. It first rejects the idea that the later narration can be identified by the later Islam of one narrator. Then it considers the argument that a hadith agreeing with the original presumption (al-bara'a al-asliyya) indicates it is later, because the one opposing it would be establishing a new ruling (ta'sis) which is better than mere emphasis (ta'kid). The text counters that this would involve abrogation by ijtihad, which is unacceptable. The Hanafis take a different approach: they prefer the hadith that opposes the original presumption, to avoid repeated lifting of rulings. They argue that this is not actual naskh but merely a tarjih for practical application. The passage emphasizes that this tarjih is sufficient for preferring the conflicting hadith.

Key Terms
البراءة الأصلية (al-bara'a al-asliyya): The original presumption that things are free from legal ruling (e.g., permissibility).
التأسيس (al-ta'sis): Establishing a new ruling that was not previously present.
التأكيد (al-ta'kid): Emphasizing a ruling already established, adding no new content.
الرفع (al-raf'): Lifting of a ruling, distinguished from naskh in this passage.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a revealed ruling by another revealed ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, why do they prefer the hadith that opposes the original presumption?
A) Because it is more likely to be from a reliable narrator
B) To avoid repeated lifting (raf')
C) Because it is confirmed by consensus
D) Because it is earlier in time

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers the hadith opposing the original presumption as a practical tarjih, not as naskh, to avoid repeated lifting and impermissible abrogation by ijtihad.

When a Companion Abandons an Unambiguous Text: Abrogation
Ruling on a Companion leaving a clear text (nass mufassar) due to abrogation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a Companion's abandonment of a mufassar text necessarily indicates knowledge of an abrogator, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-position by identifying the points of disagreement.

Core Idea
A Companion cannot abandon a clear text except due to an equally clear abrogator; his identification is binding.

The Hanafi school holds that when a Companion acts contrary to a clear, unambiguous text (nass mufassar), we cannot attribute error or ijtihad to him because the text is not open to interpretation. The only plausible explanation is that he knew of an abrogating text. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position, which does not accept Companion's identification of abrogation as automatically binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the passage to understand that we are dealing with a Companion who abandons a nass mufassar (a clear text not subject to ta'wil). The Hanafi ruling is that his knowledge of an abrogator is certain (mutayaqqan).
The text says 'تعين علمه بالناسخ' – his knowledge of the abrogator is necessarily determined.
2
Understand the reasoning chain
Why must it be abrogation? Because the Companion is too upright to commit a major sin by contradicting a clear text, and the text itself cannot be interpreted away. Therefore, abrogation is the only possibility.
The argument uses the certainty that a Companion does not knowingly oppose a clear divine command.
3
Distinguish between two possibilities: correct or mistaken identification
The Companion's knowledge could be correct (النسخ صواب) or mistaken (خطأ). The Hanafi argument eliminates the possibility of error because the abrogator of a mufassar text must itself be mufassar, hence equally clear, leaving no room for mistake.
The text says 'فإن ناسخ المفسر لا يكون إلا مثله فلا يحتمل الخطأ' – the abrogator of a mufassar can only be like it, so it cannot be erroneous.
4
Note the Shafi'i disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i does not accept that the Companion's identification is automatically binding, because in his view, the Companion might rely on a weaker abrogator or be mistaken. The Hanafi response is that the abrogator must be equally clear, so error is impossible.
The text ends with 'خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى لما ارتكز في ظنه ما مر' – contrary to al-Shafi'i due to what settled in his opinion as passed.
5
Apply the principle to a case
Consider a hypothetical: A Companion ignores the Qur'anic verse on inheritance for a childless woman and instead follows a hadith that 'We prophets do not leave inheritance.' The Hanafi rule would require us to accept that the Companion knew the verse was abrogated by that hadith, because the hadith is also clear and the Companion's character prevents sin.
This step links the theory to practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ترك الصحابي نصاً مفسراً غير قابل للتأويل تعين علمه بالناسخ لأن مخالفة المفسر عسى أن تكون كبيرة، والصحابي أن أجل من يرتكبه، ولا يحتمل التأويل حتى يكون مؤولاً، فتعين النسخ لا غير، فإما أن يكون علمه بالنسخ خطأ أو صواباً، والأول باطل كما أشار إليه بقوله : (واحتمال جعله ما ليس بناسخ في الواقع (ناسخاً أبعد من الصواب، فإن ناسخ المفسر لا يكون إلا مثله فلا يحتمل الخطأ فتعين الثاني فيجب اتباعه خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى لما ارتكز في ظنه ما مر
And if the Companion abandons a clearly expressed text (nass mufassar) not open to interpretation, then his knowledge of the abrogator is necessarily certain, because contradicting the clearly expressed text might be a major sin, and the Companion is too exalted to commit it. Nor does the text admit interpretation such that it could be interpreted [away]. So abrogation alone is determined. Then either his knowledge of abrogation is erroneous or correct. The first is invalid, as he indicated by his saying: (And the possibility of his making what is not an abrogator in reality an abrogator is farther from correctness, for the abrogator of a clearly expressed text can only be like it, so it is not open to error. So the second is necessarily true, and it is obligatory to follow him, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, because of what had settled in his opinion as mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on the authority of a Companion's practice when it contradicts a clear text (nass mufassar) that is not open to interpretation (ta'wil). Since the Companion is upright and cannot commit a major sin by knowingly opposing a clear text, the only explanation is that he knew of an abrogating verse or hadith. The Companion's identification of the abrogator is then treated as correct and binding, because the abrogator of a mufassar text must itself be equally clear, leaving no room for error. The Shafi'i position, which does not necessarily accept the Companion's understanding of abrogation, is rejected. This rule is a subset of the broader Hanafi principle of accepting a Companion's interpretive actions when they involve abandoning a clear ruling.

Key Terms
النص المفسر (an-nass al-mufassar): A text that is clear, unambiguous, and not open to interpretation or ta'wil.
النسخ (an-naskh): Abrogation, the superseding of a ruling by a later revealed text.
التأويل (at-ta'wil): Interpretation that departs from the apparent meaning, often due to contextual or linguistic considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi reasoning in this passage, why is it impossible for a Companion to erroneously identify an abrogator for a mufassar text?
A) Because Companions are infallible.
B) Because the abrogator must be equally clear (mufassar), so it cannot be mistaken.
C) Because the Companion's ijtihad is always correct.
D) Because the text itself was probably not authentic.

Key Takeaway
When a Companion abandons an unambiguous text (nass mufassar), the Hanafi school holds that the only explanation is abrogation, and the Companion's identification of the abrogator is binding and free from error.

Hujjiyyat Shari' Man Qablana: Hanafi Position on Previous Laws
Dispute on hujjiyyat shari' man qablana.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the bindingness of previous prophets' laws (shar' man qablana) and evaluate the opposing arguments using the Hanafi usul framework.

Core Idea
A previous prophet's law binds us only if confirmed by our own revelation.

This passage plunges into one of the classic usul disputes: Are the sharias of earlier prophets (shar' man qablana) legally binding upon the Muslim Ummah? The issue is not merely historical; it affects how we derive rulings from Quranic stories and how we understand the scope of naskh. The Hanafi school, represented here, argues that previous laws are hujja (proof) only when corroborated by our own revelation, not absolutely. We will trace the arguments and counter-arguments presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Dispute
First, recognize that the passage debates whether the laws of prophets before Muhammad (SAW) are binding on Muslims. The proponent (likely representing the Mutakallimun or Shafi'i view) argues they are, while the Hanafi respondent restricts this bindingness.
The dispute hinges on the example of Prophet Musa (AS) and the Prophet's (SAW) action regarding stoning.
2
Analyze the First Argument: The Musa Example
The proponent says the Prophet (SAW) was commanded to judge by Musa's law, so it must be binding. The Hanafi response: The command came through new revelation (wahi ghayr matluww) that agreed with Musa's law; the Prophet was not bound by Musa's law per se.
This shows the Hanafi principle that only our own revelation is the primary source.
3
Examine the Quranic Verses Argument
The proponent cites verses commanding to follow the religion of previous prophets. The Hanafi response restricts this to beliefs and the five universal necessities (daruriyyat), not all rulings, because some are abrogated.
This is a key limitation: the Quranic command is general but its application is specific.
4
Evaluate the Opponents' Three Objections
The opponents raise three objections: (1) Mu'adh's hadith didn't mention previous laws; (2) consensus that our sharia abrogates all previous; (3) the Prophet waited for revelation, not referring to previous scriptures. The Hanafi responses: (1) Quran encompasses previous laws or it was rare; (2) only contradictory parts are abrogated (e.g., qisas remains); (3) he did act on known previous laws (e.g., Ashura) and non-reference was due to distortion.
Each response illustrates a Hanafi usul principle: limitation of naskh, reliance on confirmed reports, and caution regarding tahrif.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: For example, the law of qisas (eye for an eye) from the Torah is binding according to Hanafis because it is confirmed in the Quran, but the opponent might say it is abrogated until a Quranic verse is found.
This shows how the dispute affects actual fiqh rulings.

وقد يقرر الجواب بأن الكتاب يشمل التوراة والإنجيل وغيرهما لأنها كتبت وإنما لم يجعل عليه كلامه بعيد خلاف المتبادر إذ المتبادر من كتاب الله في عرف الصحابة إلى الآن القرآن الشريف (أو) لم يذكره لأنه قليل جداً، وإنما ذكر ما هو منشأ أكثر الأحكام (و) قالوا: (ثانياً: الإجماع على أن شريعتنا ناسخة للشرائع التي قبلنا والتعبد بالمنسوخ حرام (قلنا) شريعتنا ناسخة (لما خالفها) من الأحكام (لا) مطلقاً) كجميعها حتى
